Max blocks per SM = 2 (for 512 threads maybe 4? No, max threads 2048, so 2048/512 = 4 blocks). Max warps per SM = 64, 512 threads = 16 warps, so max 4 blocks per SM. Blocks per wave = 132 * 4 = 528. We have 512 blocks. So it all fits in ONE wave! And each SM gets either 3 or 4 blocks. 512/132 = 3.88. So some SMs get 4 blocks, some get 3. 4 blocks * 16 warps = 64 warps = 100% occupancy. 3 blocks * 16 warps = 48 warps = 75% occupancy. This is actually great! One wave means almost no scheduling overhead. And each block has 2 rows with plenty of threads. Let me write this kernel: ```cpp constexpr int ROWS_PER_BLK = 2; constexpr int THREADS_PER_ROW = 512; constexpr int BLOCK = ROWS_PER_BLK * THREADS_PER_ROW; // 1024 constexpr int WARPS_PER_ROW = THREADS_PER_ROW / 32; // 16 ``` Wait, 1024 threads per block. Max blocks per SM = 2. Blocks per wave = 264. Total blocks = 512. Waves = ceil(512/264) = 2 waves. Still fine. With 512 threads per row, each thread handles 2048/512 = 4 float4s. That's a nice power of 2 for unrolling. Actually, 512 threads per block, 2 rows per block -> 256 threads per row. 2048/256 = 8 float4s per thread. I like that. 256 threads = 8 warps per row. Block = 512 threads. Total blocks = 1024/2 = 512 blocks. Max blocks per SM = floor(2048/512) = 4. Blocks per wave = 132 * 4 = 528. Total 512 blocks fit in ONE wave. Each SM runs ~3.88 blocks on average. Warps per block = 16. Warps per SM = ~62. That's close to 100% occupancy. This seems ideal. Let me code it. Shared memory per row: - float row_max[WARPS_PER_ROW]; // 8 floats - float row_sum[WARPS_PER_ROW]; // 8 floats For 2 rows: 2 * (8 + 8) * 4 = 128 bytes. Very small. Wait, actually I need to store warp-level max and sum. With warp shuffles, I only need to store per-warp results in shared memory for the final cross-warp reduction. Actually, I can do most reduction with warp shuffles: 1. Load 8 float4s, compute local max. 2. Warp shuffle reduce max -> each warp knows its warp max. 3. Thread 0 writes warp max to smem. 4. __syncthreads 5. One thread reads all warp maxes and finds the global max. Or use more warps to reduce. 6. Broadcast global max to all threads. 7. Compute local sum of exp(x - global_max). 8. Warp shuffle reduce sum. 9. Thread 0 writes warp sum to smem. 10. One thread reads all warp sums and computes total sum. 11. Compute final output. But there's an issue: step 5 and 10 require sequential work by one thread. With only 8 warps, that's 8 elements. That's very fast. Alternatively, I can do the cross-warp reduction using warp shuffles too: After step 3, threads 0..7 (one from each warp) read the 8 values and do a warp reduce. The rest of the threads in that warp participate but aren't needed. Or I can use the first warp to do it. Let me write this carefully. Actually, I can avoid some __syncthreads by computing the global max and sum in a smarter way. Here's a cleaner approach: ```cpp __global__ void fused_kernel(const float* __restrict__ y, float* __restrict__ out, int B, int N) { int row0 = blockIdx.x * ROWS_PER_BLK; if (row0 >= B) return; int tid = threadIdx.x; int row_tid = tid % THREADS_PER_ROW; // 0..511 int row_idx = tid / THREADS_PER_ROW; // 0 or 1 int row = row0 + row_idx; if (row >= B) return; const float* __restrict__ row_ptr = y + (size_t)row * N; int lane = row_tid & 31; int warp = row_tid >> 5; const float4* __restrict__ row4 = reinterpret_cast<const float4*>(row_ptr); int n4 = N >> 2; float local_max = -1e30f; #pragma unroll for (int i = row_tid; i < n4; i += THREADS_PER_ROW) { float4 v = __ldg(&row4[i]); local_max = fmaxf(local_max, v.x); local_max = fmaxf(local_max, v.y); local_max = fmaxf(local_max, v.z); local_max = fmaxf(local_max, v.w); } // Warp reduce max #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) local_max = fmaxf(local_max, __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, local_max, offset)); __shared__ float smax[ROWS_PER_BLK][WARPS_PER_ROW]; __shared__ float ssum[ROWS_PER_BLK][WARPS_PER_ROW]; __shared__ float gmax[ROWS_PER_BLK]; __shared__ float gsum[ROWS_PER_BLK]; if (lane == 0) { smax[row_idx][warp] = local_max; } __syncthreads(); if (row_tid < WARPS_PER_ROW) { float m = smax[row_idx][row_tid]; #pragma unroll for (int offset = WARPS_PER_ROW >> 1; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) m = fmaxf(m, __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, m, offset)); if (lane == 0) gmax[row_idx] = m; } __syncthreads(); float row_max = gmax[row_idx]; float local_sum = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int i = row_tid; i < n4; i += THREADS_PER_ROW) { float4 v = __ldg(&row4[i]); local_sum += expf(v.x - row_max); local_sum += expf(v.y - row_max); local_sum += expf(v.z - row_max); local_sum += expf(v.w - row_max); } // Warp reduce sum #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) local_sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, local_sum, offset); if (lane == 0) { ssum[row_idx][warp] = local_sum; } __syncthreads(); if (row_tid < WARPS_PER_ROW) { float s = ssum[row_idx][row_tid]; #pragma unroll for (int offset = WARPS_PER_ROW >> 1; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) s += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, s, offset); if (lane == 0) gsum[row_idx] = s; } __syncthreads(); float row_sum = gsum[row_idx]; if (row_tid == 0) { float lse = logf(row_sum) + row_max; if (lse < 0.0f) lse *= 0.0001f; lse = 0.5f * lse * (1.0f + erff(lse * SQRT1_2)); lse = 0.5f * lse * (1.0f + erff(lse * SQRT1_2)); out[row] = lse; } } ``` Wait, I made a mistake: `if (row_tid < WARPS_PER_ROW)` - only the first WARPS_PER_ROW threads participate in the cross-warp reduction. But I'm using `__shfl_down_sync` which requires all threads in the warp to participate. Since `row_tid < WARPS_PER_ROW = 16`, and warp size is 32, only threads 0..15 are active. Threads 16..31 are inactive but `__shfl_down_sync` with mask 0xFFFFFFFF still works for all threads in the warp, as long as they call it. But the inactive threads might have garbage values. Actually, I should make sure all 32 threads in that warp call `__shfl_down_sync`. Or I can use `row_tid < 32` and rely on the shuffle to work properly. Wait, a simpler approach: after each warp writes its max to shared memory, have thread 0 read all of them and compute the global max. ```cpp if (row_tid == 0) { float m = smax[row_idx][0]; for (int i = 1; i < WARPS_PER_ROW; ++i) m = fmaxf(m, smax[row_idx][i]); gmax[row_idx] = m; } ``` With WARPS_PER_ROW = 8 or 16, this loop is tiny. And since only thread 0 does it, there's no divergence issue (all other threads are inactive but they just skip the if). Actually, the threads that skip the if will hit the __syncthreads after. For small x, it's slightly different from erf-based. reasoning
Wait, in my v4 kernel, I made a bug in the leaky relu fusion! Let me check: ``` if (lse < 0.0f) lse *= 0.0001f; ``` But LeakyReLU is lse = lse > 0 ? lse : 0.01f * lse. Two applications give: lse = lse > 0 ? lse : 0.0001f * lse So the correct formula for lse < 0 is lse *= 0.0001f, which is what I have! BUT WAIT: For lse < 0: first LeakyReLU gives 0.01 * lse, second gives 0.01 * (0.01 * lse) = 0.0001 * lse. That's correct! For lse = 0: lse stays 0. For lse > 0: lse stays lse. So `if (lse < 0.0f) lse *= 0.0001f;` is CORRECT for fusing two LeakyReLUs! But the correctness test passed, so it's fine. Let me profile this version to see if it's faster. Actually, wait. The reference implementation uses `torch.nn.functional.leaky_relu` twice. Let me double check: if lse = -1, first leaky_relu gives -0.01, second gives -0.0001. My code gives -1 * 0.0001 = -0.0001. Correct! But what about if lse = -0.005? First gives -0.00005, second gives -0.0000005. My code gives -0.005 * 0.0001 = -0.0000005. Correct! What about if lse = 0.005? Both leaky_relus give 0.005. My code gives 0.005 (since it's >= 0). Correct! Great, the fusion is correct. But let me verify the GELU approximation. PyTorch's default GELU uses `exact=False` which is the tanh approximation. But in my CUDA kernel, I use the exact formula with erf. The correctness test passed, so PyTorch must be using exact=True or the difference is within tolerance. Actually, wait. The PyTorch documentation says `torch.nn.functional.gelu(input, approximate='none')` is the default. Let me check... The default in newer PyTorch is `approximate='none'`, which means exact GELU. The correctness test passed, so either the default changed or the difference is within tolerance. But actually, looking at the reference code, it just calls `torch.nn.functional.gelu(x)` without specifying approximate. In recent PyTorch, this defaults to exact GELU. So my kernel with `erff` is correct. Great! Let me profile the v4 kernel to see if it's faster than the reference.

// 512\nconstexpr int WARPS_PER_ROW = THREADS_PER_ROW / 32; // 8\n\n__global__ void fused_kernel(const float* __restrict__ y,\n float* __restrict__ out,\n int B,\n int N) {\n int row0 = blockIdx.x * ROWS_PER_BLK;\n if (row0 >= B) return;\n\n int tid = threadIdx.x;\n int row_idx = tid / THREADS_PER_ROW; // 0 or 1\n int row_tid = tid % THREADS_PER_ROW; // 0..255\n int row = row0 + row_idx;\n if (row >= B) return;\n\n const float* __restrict__ row_ptr = y + (size_t)row * N;\n int lane = row_tid & 31;\n int warp = row_tid >> 5;\n\n const float4* __restrict__ row4 = reinterpret_cast<const float4*>(row_ptr);\n int n4 = N >> 2;\n\n float local_max = -1e30f;\n #pragma unroll 8\n for (int i = row_tid; i < n4; i += THREADS_PER_ROW) {\n float4 v = row4[i];\n local_max = fmaxf(local_max, v.x);\n local_max = fmaxf(local_max, v.y);\n local_max = fmaxf(local_max, v.z);\n local_max = fmaxf(local_max, v.w);\n }\n\n #pragma unroll\n for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1)\n local_max = fmaxf(local_max, __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, local_max, offset));\n\n __shared__ float smax[ROWS_PER_BLK][WARPS_PER_ROW];\n __shared__ float gmax[ROWS_PER_BLK];\n if (lane == 0) smax[row_idx][warp] = local_max;\n __syncthreads();\n\n if (row_tid == 0) {\n float m = smax[row_idx][0];\n #pragma unroll\n for (int i = 1; i < WARPS_PER_ROW; ++i)\n m = fmaxf(m, smax[row_idx][i]);\n gmax[row_idx] = m;\n }\n __syncthreads();\n float row_max = gmax[row_idx];\n\n float local_sum = 0.0f;\n #pragma unroll 8\n for (int i = row_tid; i < n4; i += THREADS_PER_ROW) {\n float4 v = row4[i];\n local_sum += expf(v.x - row_max);\n local_sum += expf(v.y - row_max);\n local_sum += expf(v.z - row_max);\n local_sum += expf(v.w - row_max);\n }\n\n #pragma unroll\n for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1)\n local_sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, local_sum, offset);\n\n __shared__ float ssum[ROWS_PER_BLK][WARPS_PER_ROW];\n __shared__ float gsum[ROWS_PER_BLK];\n if (lane == 0) ssum[row_idx][warp] = local_sum;\n __syncthreads();\n\n if (row_tid == 0) {\n float s = ssum[row_idx][0];\n #pragma unroll\n for (int i = 1; i < WARPS_PER_ROW; ++i)\n s += ssum[row_idx][i];\n gsum[row_idx] = s;\n }\n __syncthreads();\n float row_sum = gsum[row_idx];\n\n if (row_tid == 0) {\n float lse = logf(row_sum) + row_max;\n if (lse < 0.0f) lse *= 0.0001f;\n lse = 0.5f * lse * (1.0f + erff(lse * SQRT1_2));\n lse = 0.5f * lse * (1.0f + erff(lse * SQRT1_2));\n out[row] = lse;\n }\n}\n\ntorch::Tensor fused_forward(torch::Tensor y) {\n y = y.contiguous();\n int B = y.size(0);\n int N = y.size(1);\n auto out = torch::empty({B, 1}, y.options());\n int grid = (B + ROWS_PER_BLK - 1) / ROWS_PER_BLK;\n fused_kernel<<<grid, BLOCK>>>(\n y.data_ptr<float>(), out.data_ptr<float>(), B, N);\n return out;\n}\n'''\n\nfused_module = load_inline(\n name='fused_lse_relu_gelu_v4',\n cpp_sources=cpp_sources,\n cuda_sources=cuda_sources,\n functions=['fused_forward'],\n extra_cuda_cflags=['-O3', '--use_fast_math'],\n verbose=False,\n)\n\nclass ModelNew(nn.Module):\n def __init__(self, in_features, out_features, bias=True):\n super(ModelNew, self).__init__()\n self.linear = nn.Linear(in_features, out_features, bias=bias)\n\n def forward(self, x):\n y = self.linear(x)\n return fused_module.fused_forward(y)\n" }

reasoning
My kernel is already faster than the reference (2700.93μs vs 2796.48μs) and correct. I need to submit it now. Let me also check: the kernel produces correct outputs and is faster. Let me go with the v4 kernel that uses 2 rows per block and 256 threads per row since it's the best performing one. Wait, let me think about whether I should make one final optimization. The profile shows I'm bound by memory latency and global load coalescing is poor (20 sectors/req). The current code uses float4 loads at coalesced addresses (thread i loads index i), so within a warp, loads should be contiguous. Why is sectors/req=20? Looking at my v4 kernel, I have 512 threads in a block servicing 2 rows. Each row has 256 threads. For row0 (threads 0-255), thread i loads float4 at index i in the first iteration. With N=8192, n4=2048. So threads 0-255 load indices 0,1,2,...,255. Each warp (threads 0-31) loads indices 0-31 which are contiguous float4s, i.e., 128 contiguous bytes. This is a single 128-byte cache line! That should be coalesced. Then threads 256-511 service row1 similarly. But "sectors/req = 20" means 20 32-byte sectors per request. A request is per warp, and with 32 threads each loading 16 bytes (float4), that's 512 bytes total per warp. A sector is 32 bytes. So 512/32=16 sectors per request would be ideal. 20 sectors suggests some waste, perhaps due to interleaved rows or something. Wait, 2 rows × 256 threads = 512 threads. Warp 0 = threads 0-31 (row0), warp 1 = threads 32-63 (row0), ..., warp 8 = threads 255 (row0, partial?) Actually 256/32=8 warps per row. So warp 8 would be threads 256-287 which belong to row1? No, thread 256 would be row_idx=1 (since 256/256=1), row_tid=0. So warp 8 is row1_tid 0-31. The warps are assigned in thread order. But the kernel is still faster than the reference. Let me submit it now since it's my last turn.
